</tr><tr><th style="padding:0.1em;text-align: left; background:#ccf; padding: 2px; border-bottom: 2px solid #f9f9f9;">
<a href="/wiki/Percussion" class="mw-redirect" title="Percussion">Percussion</a></th></tr><tr><th style="padding:0.1em;text-align: left; background:#ccf; padding: 2px; border-bottom: 2px solid #f9f9f9;">
<a href="/wiki/Keyboard_instrument" title="Keyboard instrument">Keyboards</a></th></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Musical_instruments_sidebar" title="Template:Musical instruments sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Musical_instruments_sidebar" title="Template talk:Musical instruments sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Musical_instruments_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>clarinet</b> is a family of <a href="/wiki/Woodwind_instrument" title="Woodwind instrument">woodwind instruments</a>. It has a <a href="/wiki/Single-reed_instrument" title="Single-reed instrument">single-reed</a> mouthpiece, a straight, cylindrical tube with an almost cylindrical <a href="/wiki/Bore_(wind_instruments)" title="Bore (wind instruments)">bore</a>, and a flared bell. A person who plays a clarinet is called a <i><a href="/wiki/Clarinetist" class="mw-redirect" title="Clarinetist">clarinetist</a></i> (sometimes spelled <i>clarinettist</i>).
</p><p>While the similarity in sound between the earliest clarinets and the trumpet may hold a clue to its name,  other factors may have been involved. During the <a href="/wiki/Baroque_music" title="Baroque music">Late Baroque era</a>, composers such as <a href="/wiki/Bach" class="mw-redirect" title="Bach">Bach</a> and <a href="/wiki/Handel" class="mw-redirect" title="Handel">Handel</a> were making new demands on the skills of their trumpeters, who were often required to play difficult melodic passages in the high, or as it came to be called, <i>clarion</i> register. Since the trumpets of this time had no <a href="/wiki/Brass_instrument_valve" title="Brass instrument valve">valves or pistons</a>, melodic passages would often require the use of the highest part of the trumpet's range, where the harmonics were close enough together to produce <a href="/wiki/Scale_(music)" title="Scale (music)">scales</a> of adjacent notes as opposed to the gapped scales or <a href="/wiki/Arpeggios" class="mw-redirect" title="Arpeggios">arpeggios</a> of the lower register. The trumpet parts that required this specialty were known by the term <i>clarino</i> and this in turn came to apply to the musicians themselves. It is probable that the term clarinet may stem from the diminutive version of the 'clarion' or 'clarino' and it has been suggested that clarino players may have helped themselves out by playing particularly difficult passages on these newly developed "mock trumpets".<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Johann_Christoph_Denner" title="Johann Christoph Denner">Johann Christoph Denner</a> is generally believed to have invented the clarinet in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> around the year 1700 by adding a <a href="/wiki/Register_key" title="Register key">register key</a> to the earlier <a href="/wiki/Chalumeau" title="Chalumeau">chalumeau</a>, usually in the key of C. Over time, additional keywork and airtight pads were added to improve the tone and playability.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In modern times, the most popular clarinet is the B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> clarinet. However, the clarinet in A, just a <a href="/wiki/Semitone" title="Semitone">semitone</a> lower, is regularly used in orchestral music. An orchestral clarinetist must own both a clarinet in A and B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> since the repertoire is divided fairly evenly between the two. Since the middle of the 19th century the <a href="/wiki/Bass_clarinet" title="Bass clarinet">bass clarinet</a> (nowadays invariably in B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> but with extra keys to extend the register down a few notes) has become an essential addition to the orchestra. The <a href="/wiki/Clarinet_family" title="Clarinet family">clarinet family</a> ranges from the (extremely rare) BBB<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> <a href="/wiki/Subcontrabass_clarinet" class="mw-redirect" title="Subcontrabass clarinet">octo-contrabass</a> to the A<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> <a href="/wiki/Piccolo_clarinet" class="mw-redirect" title="Piccolo clarinet">piccolo clarinet</a>. The clarinet has proved to be an exceptionally flexible instrument, used in the <a href="/wiki/Classical_music" title="Classical music">classical</a> repertoire as in <a href="/wiki/Concert_band" title="Concert band">concert bands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Military_band" title="Military band">military bands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marching_band" title="Marching band">marching bands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Klezmer" title="Klezmer">klezmer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a>, and other styles.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clarinet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The word <i>clarinet</i> may have entered the English language via the <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> <i>clarinette</i> (the feminine diminutive of <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a> <i>clarin</i> or <i>clarion</i>), or from <a href="/wiki/Occitan_language" title="Occitan language">Provençal</a> <i><a href="https://oc.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clarin" class="extiw" title="oc:Clarin">clarin</a></i>, "oboe".<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It would seem however that its real roots are to be found amongst some of the various names for trumpets used around the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baroque" title="Baroque">Baroque</a> eras. <i>Clarion</i>, <i>clarin</i>, and the Italian <i>clarino</i> are all derived from the medieval term <i>claro</i>, which referred to an early form of trumpet.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> This is probably the origin of the Italian <i>clarinetto</i>, itself a diminutive of <i>clarino</i>, and consequently of the European equivalents such as <i>clarinette</i> in French or the German <i>Klarinette</i>. According to <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottfried_Walther" title="Johann Gottfried Walther">Johann Gottfried Walther</a>, writing in 1732, the reason for the name is that "it sounded from far off not unlike a trumpet". The English form <i>clarinet</i> is found as early as 1733, and the now-archaic <i>clarionet</i> appears from 1784 until the early years of the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clarinet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sound">Sound</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clarinet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Sound">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Cylinder_(geometry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cylinder (geometry)">cylindrical</a> bore is primarily responsible for the clarinet's distinctive <a href="/wiki/Timbre" title="Timbre">timbre</a>, which varies between its three main <a href="/wiki/Register_(music)" title="Register (music)">registers</a>, known as the <i>chalumeau</i>, <i>clarion</i>, and <i>altissimo</i>. The tone quality can vary greatly with the clarinetist, music, instrument, mouthpiece, and reed. The differences in instruments and geographical isolation of clarinetists led to the development from the last part of the 18th century onwards of several different schools of playing. The most prominent were the German/Viennese traditions and French school. The latter was centered on the clarinetists of the <a href="/wiki/Conservatoire_de_Paris" title="Conservatoire de Paris">Conservatoire de Paris</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The proliferation of recorded music has made examples of different styles of playing available. The modern clarinetist has a diverse palette of "acceptable" tone qualities to choose from.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Clarinette_basse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Clarinette_basse.jpg/100px-Clarinette_basse.jpg" decoding="async" width="100" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Clarinette_basse.jpg/150px-Clarinette_basse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Clarinette_basse.jpg/200px-Clarinette_basse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="284" data-file-height="639" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Clarinette_basse.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bass clarinet</div></div></div>
<p>The A and B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> clarinets have nearly the same bore and use the same mouthpiece.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Orchestral clarinetists using the A and B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> instruments in a concert could use the same mouthpiece (and often the same barrel) (see 'usage' below). The A and B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> have nearly identical tonal quality, although the A typically has a slightly warmer sound. The tone of the <a href="/wiki/E-flat_clarinet" title="E-flat clarinet">E<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> clarinet</a> is brighter and can be heard even through loud orchestral or concert band textures.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Bass_clarinet" title="Bass clarinet">bass clarinet</a> has a characteristically deep, mellow sound, while the <a href="/wiki/Alto_clarinet" title="Alto clarinet">alto clarinet</a> is similar in tone to the bass (though not as dark).<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Range">Range</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clarinet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Range">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Clarinet_family" title="Clarinet family">clarinet family</a>, <a href="/wiki/E-flat_clarinet" title="E-flat clarinet">E-flat clarinet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soprano_clarinet" title="Soprano clarinet">soprano clarinet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alto_clarinet" title="Alto clarinet">alto clarinet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bass_clarinet" title="Bass clarinet">bass clarinet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Basset-horn" class="mw-redirect" title="Basset-horn">basset-horn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Contra-alto_clarinet" class="mw-redirect" title="Contra-alto clarinet">contra-alto clarinet</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Contrabass_clarinet" title="Contrabass clarinet">contrabass clarinet</a></div>
<p>Clarinets have the largest pitch <a href="/wiki/Range_(music)" title="Range (music)">range</a> of common woodwinds.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The intricate <a href="/wiki/Key_(instrument)" title="Key (instrument)">key</a> organization that makes this possible can make the playability of some passages awkward. The bottom of the clarinet's written range is defined by the keywork on each instrument, standard keywork schemes allowing a low E on the common B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> clarinet. The lowest <a href="/wiki/Concert_pitch" title="Concert pitch">concert pitch</a> depends on the <a href="/wiki/Transposing_instrument" title="Transposing instrument">transposition</a> of the instrument in question. The nominal highest note of the B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> clarinet is a semitone higher than the highest note of the <a href="/wiki/Oboe" title="Oboe">oboe</a>. Since the clarinet has a wider range of notes, the lowest note of the B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> clarinet is significantly deeper (a minor or major sixth) than the lowest note of the oboe.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nearly all <a href="/wiki/Soprano_clarinet" title="Soprano clarinet">soprano</a> and <a href="/wiki/Piccolo_clarinet" class="mw-redirect" title="Piccolo clarinet">piccolo clarinets</a> have keywork enabling them to play the E below middle C as their lowest written note (in <a href="/wiki/Scientific_pitch_notation" title="Scientific pitch notation">scientific pitch notation</a> that sounds D<sub>3</sub> on a soprano clarinet or C<sub>4</sub>, i.e. concert middle C, on a piccolo clarinet), though some B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> clarinets go down to E<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span><sub>3</sub> to enable them to match the range of the A clarinet.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> On the B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> soprano clarinet, the concert pitch of the lowest note is D<sub>3</sub>, a <a href="/wiki/Whole_tone" class="mw-redirect" title="Whole tone">whole tone</a> lower than the written pitch. Most alto and bass clarinets have an extra key to allow a (written) E<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span><sub>3</sub>. Modern professional-quality bass clarinets generally have additional keywork to written C<sub>3</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Among the less commonly encountered members of the clarinet family, <a href="/wiki/Contra-alto_clarinet" class="mw-redirect" title="Contra-alto clarinet">contra-alto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Contrabass_clarinet" title="Contrabass clarinet">contrabass clarinets</a> may have keywork to written E<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span><sub>3</sub>, D<sub>3</sub>, or C<sub>3</sub>;<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Basset_clarinet" title="Basset clarinet">basset clarinet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Basset_horn" title="Basset horn">basset horn</a> generally go to low C<sub>3</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Defining the top end of a clarinet's range is difficult, since many advanced players can produce notes well above the highest notes commonly found in method books. G<sub>6</sub> is usually the highest note clarinetists encounter in classical repertoire.<sup id="cite_ref-lowry_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lowry-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The C above that (C<sub>7</sub> i.e. resting on the fifth ledger line above the treble staff) is attainable by advanced players and is shown on many <a href="/wiki/Fingering_(music)" title="Fingering (music)">fingering</a> charts,<sup id="cite_ref-lowry_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lowry-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> and fingerings as high as A<sub>7</sub> exist.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The range of a clarinet can be divided into three distinct <a href="/wiki/Register_(music)" title="Register (music)">registers</a>:
</p>
<ul><li>The lowest register, from low written E to the written B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> above middle C (B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span><sub>4</sub>), is known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Chalumeau" title="Chalumeau">chalumeau</a></i> register (named after the instrument that was the clarinet's immediate predecessor).</li>
<li>The middle register is known as the <i>clarion</i> register (sometimes in the U.S. as the <i>clarino</i> register from the Italian) <sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> and spans just over an octave (from written B above middle C (B<sub>4</sub>) to the C two octaves above middle C (C<sub>6</sub>));<sup id="cite_ref-lowry_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lowry-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> it is the dominant range for most members of the clarinet family.</li>
<li>The top or <i><a href="/wiki/Altissimo" title="Altissimo">altissimo</a></i> register consists of the notes above the written C two octaves above middle C (C<sub>6</sub>).<sup id="cite_ref-lowry_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lowry-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>All three registers have characteristically different sounds. The chalumeau register is rich and dark. The clarion register is brighter and sweet, like a trumpet (<i>clarion</i>) heard from afar. The altissimo register can be piercing and sometimes shrill.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acoustics">Acoustics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clarinet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Acoustics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sound_wave_propagation_in_the_soprano_clarinet.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Sound_wave_propagation_in_the_soprano_clarinet.jpg/220px-Sound_wave_propagation_in_the_soprano_clarinet.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Sound_wave_propagation_in_the_soprano_clarinet.jpg/330px-Sound_wave_propagation_in_the_soprano_clarinet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Sound_wave_propagation_in_the_soprano_clarinet.jpg/440px-Sound_wave_propagation_in_the_soprano_clarinet.jpg 2x" data-file-width="448" data-file-height="610" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sound_wave_propagation_in_the_soprano_clarinet.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sound wave propagation in the soprano clarinet</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sound" title="Sound">Sound</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Wave" title="Wave">wave</a> that propagates through the air as a result of a local variation in <a href="/wiki/Air_pressure" class="mw-redirect" title="Air pressure">air pressure</a>. The production of sound by a clarinet follows these steps:<sup id="cite_ref-physics_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-physics-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>The mouthpiece and reed are surrounded by the player's lips, which put light, even pressure on the reed and form an airtight seal. Air is blown past the reed and down the instrument. In the same way a flag flaps in the breeze, the air rushing past the reed causes it to vibrate. As air pressure from the mouth increases, the amount the reed vibrates increases until the reed hits the mouthpiece.<br />At this point, the reed stays pressed against the mouthpiece until either the springiness of the reed forces it to open or a returning pressure wave 'bumps' into the reed and opens it. Each time the reed opens, a puff of air goes through the gap, after which the reed swings shut again. When played loudly, the reed can spend up to 50% of the time shut.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The 'puff of air' or <a href="/wiki/Compression_wave" class="mw-redirect" title="Compression wave">compression wave</a> (around 3% greater pressure than the surrounding air<sup id="cite_ref-physics_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-physics-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>) travels down the cylindrical tube and escapes at the point where the tube opens out. This is either at the closest open hole or at the end of the tube (see diagram: image 1).</li>
<li>More than a 'neutral' amount of air escapes from the instrument, which creates a slight vacuum or <a href="/wiki/Rarefaction" title="Rarefaction">rarefaction</a> in the clarinet tube. This rarefaction wave travels back up the tube (image 2).</li>
<li>The rarefaction is reflected off the sloping end wall of the clarinet mouthpiece. The opening between the reed and the mouthpiece makes very little difference to the reflection of the rarefaction wave. This is because the opening is very small compared to the size of the tube, so almost the entire wave is reflected back down the tube even if the reed is completely open at the time the wave hits (image 3).</li>
<li>When the rarefaction wave reaches the other (open) end of the tube, air rushes in to fill the slight vacuum. A little more than a 'neutral' amount of air enters the tube and causes a compression wave to travel back up the tube (image 4). Once the compression wave reaches the mouthpiece end of the 'tube', it is reflected again back down the pipe. However at this point, either because the compression wave 'bumped' the reed or because of the natural vibration cycle of the reed, the gap opens and another 'puff' of air is sent down the pipe.</li>
<li>The original compression wave, now greatly reinforced by the second 'puff' of air, sets off on another two trips down the pipe (travelling 4 pipe lengths in total) before the cycle is repeated again.</li></ol>
<p>The cycle repeats at a frequency relative to how long it takes a wave to travel to the first open hole and back twice (i.e. four times the length of the pipe). For example: when all the holes bar the very top one are open (i.e. the trill 'B' key is pressed), the note <a href="/wiki/A440_(pitch_standard)" title="A440 (pitch standard)">A4</a> (440 <a href="/wiki/Hertz" title="Hertz">Hz</a>) is produced. This represents a repeat of the cycle 440 times per second.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to this primary compression wave, other waves, known as <a href="/wiki/Harmonics" class="mw-redirect" title="Harmonics">harmonics</a>, are created. Harmonics are caused by factors including the imperfect wobbling and shaking of the reed, the reed sealing the mouthpiece opening for part of the wave cycle (which creates a flattened section of the sound wave), and imperfections (bumps and holes) in the bore. A wide variety of compression waves are created, but only some (primarily the odd harmonics) are reinforced. These extra waves are what gives the clarinet its characteristic tone.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Bore_(wind_instruments)" title="Bore (wind instruments)">bore</a> is <a href="/wiki/Cylinder" title="Cylinder">cylindrical</a> for most of the tube with an inner bore diameter between 14 and 15.5 millimetres (0.55 and 0.61&#160;in), but there is a subtle <a href="/wiki/Hourglass" title="Hourglass">hourglass</a> shape, with the thinnest part below the junction between the upper and lower joint.<sup id="cite_ref-baines_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baines-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The reduction is 1 to 3 millimetres (0.039 to 0.118&#160;in) depending on the maker. This hourglass shape, although invisible to the naked eye, helps to correct the pitch/scale discrepancy between the chalumeau and clarion registers (perfect <a href="/wiki/Twelfth_(interval)" class="mw-redirect" title="Twelfth (interval)">twelfth</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-baines_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baines-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The diameter of the bore affects characteristics such as available <a href="/wiki/Harmonic" title="Harmonic">harmonics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timbre" title="Timbre">timbre</a>, and pitch stability (how far the player can bend a note in the manner required in jazz and other music). The bell at the bottom of the clarinet flares out to improve the tone and tuning of the lowest notes.
</p><p>Most modern clarinets have "undercut" <a href="/wiki/Tone_hole" title="Tone hole">tone holes</a> that improve intonation and sound. Undercutting means <a href="/wiki/Chamfer" title="Chamfer">chamfering</a> the bottom edge of tone holes inside the bore. Acoustically, this makes the tone hole function as if it were larger, but its main function is to allow the air column to follow the curve up through the tone hole (surface tension) instead of "blowing past" it under the increasingly directional frequencies of the upper registers.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The fixed reed and fairly uniform diameter of the clarinet give the instrument an acoustical behavior approximating that of a cylindrical <a href="/wiki/Stopped_pipe" class="mw-redirect" title="Stopped pipe">stopped pipe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-physics_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-physics-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Recorder_(musical_instrument)" title="Recorder (musical instrument)">Recorders</a> use a tapered internal bore to <a href="/wiki/Overblowing" title="Overblowing">overblow</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Octave" title="Octave">octave</a> when the thumb/register hole is pinched open, while the clarinet, with its cylindrical bore, overblows at the <a href="/wiki/Twelfth_(interval)" class="mw-redirect" title="Twelfth (interval)">twelfth</a>. Adjusting the angle of the bore taper controls the frequencies of the overblown notes (harmonics).<sup id="cite_ref-physics_20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-physics-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Changing the mouthpiece's tip opening and the length of the reed changes aspects of the harmonic timbre or voice of the clarinet because this changes the speed of reed vibrations.<sup id="cite_ref-physics_20-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-physics-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Generally, the goal of the clarinetist when producing a sound is to make as much of the reed vibrate as possible, making the sound fuller, warmer, and potentially louder.
</p><p>The lip position and pressure, shaping of the vocal tract, choice of reed and mouthpiece, amount of air pressure created, and evenness of the airflow account for most of the clarinetist's ability to control the tone of a clarinet.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> A highly skilled clarinetist will provide the ideal lip and air pressure for each frequency (note) being produced. They will have an <a href="/wiki/Embouchure" title="Embouchure">embouchure</a> which places an even pressure across the reed by carefully controlling their lip muscles. The airflow will also be carefully controlled by using the strong stomach muscles (as opposed to the weaker and erratic chest muscles) and they will use the diaphragm to oppose the stomach muscles to achieve a tone softer than a forte rather than weakening the stomach muscle tension to lower air pressure.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Their vocal tract will be shaped to resonate at frequencies associated with the tone being produced.
</p><p>Covering or uncovering the tone holes varies the length of the pipe, changing the <a href="/wiki/Acoustic_resonance" title="Acoustic resonance">resonant frequencies</a> of the enclosed air column and hence the <a href="/wiki/Pitch_(music)" title="Pitch (music)">pitch</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-physics_20-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-physics-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> A clarinetist moves between the chalumeau and clarion registers through use of the <a href="/wiki/Register_key" title="Register key">register key</a>; clarinetists call the change from chalumeau register to clarion register "the break".<sup id="cite_ref-pinksterboer_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pinksterboer-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The open register key stops the fundamental frequency from being reinforced, and the reed is forced to vibrate at three times the speed it was originally. This produces a note a twelfth above the original note.
</p><p>Most instruments <a href="/wiki/Overblow" class="mw-redirect" title="Overblow">overblow</a> at two times the speed of the fundamental frequency (the octave), but as the clarinet acts as a closed pipe system, the reed cannot vibrate at twice its original speed because it would be creating a 'puff' of air at the time the previous 'puff' is returning as a rarefaction. This means it cannot be reinforced and so would die away. The chalumeau register plays fundamentals, whereas the clarion register, aided by the register key, plays third harmonics (a perfect twelfth higher than the fundamentals). The first several notes of the altissimo range, aided by the register key and venting with the first left-hand hole, play fifth harmonics (a major seventeenth, a perfect twelfth plus a major sixth, above the fundamentals). The clarinet is therefore said to overblow at the twelfth and, when moving to the altissimo register, seventeenth.
</p><p>By contrast, nearly all other woodwind instruments overblow at the octave or (like the <a href="/wiki/Ocarina" title="Ocarina">ocarina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tonette" title="Tonette">tonette</a>) do not overblow at all. A clarinet must have holes and keys for nineteen notes, a chromatic octave and a half from bottom E to B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span>, in its lowest register to play the chromatic scale. This overblowing behavior explains the clarinet's great <a href="/wiki/Range_(music)" title="Range (music)">range</a> and complex <a href="/wiki/Fingering_(music)" title="Fingering (music)">fingering system</a>. The fifth and seventh harmonics are also available, sounding a further <a href="/wiki/Interval_(music)" title="Interval (music)">sixth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fourth_(interval)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fourth (interval)">fourth</a> (a flat, diminished fifth) higher respectively; these are the notes of the altissimo register.<sup id="cite_ref-physics_20-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-physics-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> This is also why the inner "waist" measurement is so critical to these harmonic frequencies.
</p><p>The highest notes can have a shrill, piercing quality and can be difficult to tune accurately. Different instruments often play differently in this respect due to the sensitivity of the bore and reed measurements. Using alternate fingerings and adjusting the embouchure help correct the pitch of these notes.
</p><p>Since approximately 1850, clarinets have been nominally tuned according to twelve-tone <a href="/wiki/Equal_temperament" title="Equal temperament">equal temperament</a>. Older clarinets were nominally tuned to <a href="/wiki/Meantone" class="mw-redirect" title="Meantone">meantone</a>. A skilled performer can use his or her <a href="/wiki/Embouchure" title="Embouchure">embouchure</a> to considerably alter the tuning of individual notes or produce <a href="/wiki/Vibrato" title="Vibrato">vibrato</a>, a pulsating change of pitch often employed in <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vibrato" title="Vibrato">Vibrato</a> is rare in classical or concert band literature; however, certain clarinetists, such as <a href="/wiki/Richard_Stoltzman" title="Richard Stoltzman">Richard Stoltzman</a>, use vibrato in classical music. Special fingerings may be used to play <a href="/wiki/Quarter_tone" title="Quarter tone">quarter tones</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Microtonal_music" title="Microtonal music">microtonal</a> intervals.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Around 1900, Dr. Richard H. Stein, a <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a> musicologist, made a quarter-tone clarinet, which was soon abandoned.<sup id="cite_ref-jl_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jl-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Years later, another German, Fritz Schüller of <a href="/wiki/Markneukirchen" title="Markneukirchen">Markneukirchen</a>, built a <a href="/wiki/Quarter_tone_clarinet" title="Quarter tone clarinet">quarter tone clarinet</a>, with two parallel bores of slightly different lengths whose tone holes are operated using the same keywork and a valve to switch from one bore to the other.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:QuartertoneClarinet.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/QuartertoneClarinet.jpg/150px-QuartertoneClarinet.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/QuartertoneClarinet.jpg/225px-QuartertoneClarinet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/QuartertoneClarinet.jpg/300px-QuartertoneClarinet.jpg 2x" data-file-width="422" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:QuartertoneClarinet.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Schüller's quarter-tone clarinet</div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Construction">Construction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clarinet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Construction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Clarinet_construction.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Clarinet_construction.JPG/350px-Clarinet_construction.JPG" decoding="async" width="350" height="473" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Clarinet_construction.JPG/525px-Clarinet_construction.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f0/Clarinet_construction.JPG 2x" data-file-width="530" data-file-height="716" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Clarinet_construction.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The construction of a clarinet (<a href="/wiki/Oehler_system" title="Oehler system">Oehler system</a>)</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Materials">Materials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clarinet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Materials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Clarinet bodies have been made from a variety of materials including wood, plastic, <a href="/wiki/Ebonite" title="Ebonite">hard rubber</a>, metal, <a href="/wiki/Resin" title="Resin">resin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ivory" title="Ivory">ivory</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The vast majority of clarinets used by professionals are made from <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">African</a> <a href="/wiki/Hardwood" title="Hardwood">hardwood</a>, <a href="/wiki/African_Blackwood" class="mw-redirect" title="African Blackwood">mpingo (African Blackwood)</a> or <a href="/wiki/Grenadilla" class="mw-redirect" title="Grenadilla">grenadilla</a>, rarely (because of diminishing supplies) <a href="/wiki/Rosewood_(timber)" class="mw-redirect" title="Rosewood (timber)">Honduran rosewood</a>, and sometimes even <a href="/wiki/Cocobolo" title="Cocobolo">cocobolo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Historically other woods, notably <a href="/wiki/Buxus" title="Buxus">boxwood</a>, were used.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Most inexpensive clarinets are made of plastic resin, such as <a href="/wiki/Acrylonitrile_butadiene_styrene" title="Acrylonitrile butadiene styrene">ABS</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> <i>Resonite</i> is <a href="/wiki/The_Selmer_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="The Selmer Company">Selmer</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Trademark" title="Trademark">trademark</a> name for its type of plastic. Metal soprano clarinets were popular in the early 20th century until plastic instruments supplanted them;<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> metal construction is still used for the bodies of some contra-alto and contrabass clarinets and the necks and bells of nearly all alto and larger clarinets.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Ivory was used for a few 18th-century clarinets, but it tends to crack and does not keep its shape well.<sup id="cite_ref-hoeprich_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoeprich-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Buffet_Crampon" title="Buffet Crampon">Buffet Crampon</a>'s Greenline clarinets are made from a composite of grenadilla wood powder and carbon fiber.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Such clarinets are less affected by humidity and temperature changes than wooden instruments but are heavier. Hard rubber, such as <a href="/wiki/Ebonite" title="Ebonite">ebonite</a>, has been used for clarinets since the 1860s, although few modern clarinets are made of it. Clarinet designers Alastair Hanson and Tom Ridenour are strong advocates of hard rubber.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hanson_Clarinet_Company" title="Hanson Clarinet Company">Hanson Clarinets</a> of England manufactures clarinets using a grenadilla compound reinforced with ebonite, known as 'BTR' (bithermal reinforced) grenadilla. This material is also not affected by humidity, and the weight is the same as that of a wooden clarinet.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Mouthpiece_(woodwind)" title="Mouthpiece (woodwind)">Mouthpieces</a> are generally made of hard rubber, although some inexpensive mouthpieces may be made of plastic. Other materials such as crystal/glass, wood, ivory, and metal have also been used.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ligature_(musical_instrument)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ligature (musical instrument)">Ligatures</a> are often made of metal and plated in nickel, silver, or gold. Other materials include wire, wire mesh, plastic, <a href="/wiki/Naugahyde" title="Naugahyde">naugahyde</a>, string, or leather.<sup id="cite_ref-pinksterboer_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pinksterboer-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reed">Reed</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clarinet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Reed">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The clarinet uses a single <a href="/wiki/Reed_(instrument)" class="mw-redirect" title="Reed (instrument)">reed</a> made from the cane of <i><a href="/wiki/Arundo_donax" title="Arundo donax">Arundo donax</a></i>, a type of grass.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Reeds may also be manufactured from synthetic materials. The <a href="/wiki/Ligature_(musical_instrument)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ligature (musical instrument)">ligature</a> fastens the reed to the mouthpiece. When air is blown through the opening between the reed and the mouthpiece facing, the reed vibrates and produces the clarinet's sound.
</p><p>Basic reed measurements are as follows: tip, 12 millimetres (0.47&#160;in) wide; lay, 15 millimetres (0.59&#160;in) long (distance from the place where the reed touches the mouthpiece to the tip); gap, 1 millimetre (0.039&#160;in) (distance between the underside of the reed tip and the mouthpiece). Adjustment to these measurements is one method of affecting tone color.<sup id="cite_ref-baines_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baines-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most clarinetists buy manufactured reeds, although many make adjustments to these reeds, and some make their own reeds from cane "blanks".<sup id="cite_ref-intravaia_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-intravaia-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Reeds come in varying degrees of hardness, generally indicated on a scale from one (soft) through five (hard). This numbering system is not standardized—reeds with the same number often vary in hardness across manufacturers and models.<sup id="cite_ref-pinksterboer_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pinksterboer-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Reed and mouthpiece characteristics work together to determine ease of playability, pitch stability, and tonal characteristics.<sup id="cite_ref-pinksterboer_28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pinksterboer-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Components">Components</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clarinet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Components">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Note: A <a href="/wiki/B%C3%B6hm_system_(clarinet)" class="mw-redirect" title="Böhm system (clarinet)">Böhm system</a> soprano clarinet is shown in the photos illustrating this section. However, all modern clarinets have similar components.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Selmer-clarinet-mouthpiece-reed-and-vandoren-ligature.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Selmer-clarinet-mouthpiece-reed-and-vandoren-ligature.jpg/200px-Selmer-clarinet-mouthpiece-reed-and-vandoren-ligature.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Selmer-clarinet-mouthpiece-reed-and-vandoren-ligature.jpg/300px-Selmer-clarinet-mouthpiece-reed-and-vandoren-ligature.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Selmer-clarinet-mouthpiece-reed-and-vandoren-ligature.jpg/400px-Selmer-clarinet-mouthpiece-reed-and-vandoren-ligature.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Selmer-clarinet-mouthpiece-reed-and-vandoren-ligature.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Clarinet <a href="/wiki/Reed_(music)" class="mw-redirect" title="Reed (music)">reed</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mouthpiece_(woodwind)" title="Mouthpiece (woodwind)">mouthpiece</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vandoren" title="Vandoren">Vandoren</a> <a href="/wiki/Ligature_(musical_instrument)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ligature (musical instrument)">ligature</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Reed_(music)" class="mw-redirect" title="Reed (music)">reed</a></i> is attached to the <i><a href="/wiki/Mouthpiece_(woodwind)" title="Mouthpiece (woodwind)">mouthpiece</a></i> by the <i><a href="/wiki/Ligature_(musical_instrument)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ligature (musical instrument)">ligature</a></i>, and the top half-inch or so of this assembly is held in the player's mouth. In the past, clarinetists used to wrap a string around the mouthpiece and reed instead of using a ligature. The formation of the mouth around the mouthpiece and reed is called the <i><a href="/wiki/Embouchure" title="Embouchure">embouchure</a></i>.
</p>
<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small noprint" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:ClarinetReed.ogg" title="File:ClarinetReed.ogg">B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> Clarinet reed and mouthpiece.</a></div>
<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="2.5309750566893" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="ClarinetReed.ogg" data-mwprovider="local"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/transcoded/6/6a/ClarinetReed.ogg/ClarinetReed.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="107144" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/6/6a/ClarinetReed.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (147 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="146685" /></audio></div></div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0"></div></div></td></tr>
<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>The reed is on the underside of the mouthpiece, pressing against the player's lower lip, while the top teeth normally contact the top of the mouthpiece (some players roll the upper lip under the top teeth to form what is called a 'double-lip' embouchure).<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Adjustments in the strength and shape of the embouchure change the tone and intonation (tuning). It is not uncommon for clarinetists to employ methods to relieve the pressure on the upper teeth and inner lower lip by attaching pads to the top of the mouthpiece or putting (temporary) padding on the front lower teeth, commonly from folded paper.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Buffet-R13-Clarinet-barrel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Buffet-R13-Clarinet-barrel.jpg/200px-Buffet-R13-Clarinet-barrel.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Buffet-R13-Clarinet-barrel.jpg/300px-Buffet-R13-Clarinet-barrel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Buffet-R13-Clarinet-barrel.jpg/400px-Buffet-R13-Clarinet-barrel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Buffet-R13-Clarinet-barrel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Barrel of a B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> soprano clarinet</div></div></div>
<p>Next is the short <i>barrel</i>; this part of the instrument may be extended to fine-tune the clarinet. As the pitch of the clarinet is fairly temperature-sensitive, some instruments have interchangeable barrels whose lengths vary slightly. Additional compensation for pitch variation and tuning can be made by pulling out the barrel and thus increasing the instrument's length, particularly common in group playing in which clarinets are tuned to other instruments (such as in an <a href="/wiki/Orchestra" title="Orchestra">orchestra</a> or <a href="/wiki/Concert_band" title="Concert band">concert band</a>). Some performers use a plastic barrel with a thumbwheel that adjusts the barrel length. On basset horns and lower clarinets, the barrel is normally replaced by a curved metal neck.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Buffet-R13-Clarinet-upper-joint.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Buffet-R13-Clarinet-upper-joint.jpg/200px-Buffet-R13-Clarinet-upper-joint.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Buffet-R13-Clarinet-upper-joint.jpg/300px-Buffet-R13-Clarinet-upper-joint.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Buffet-R13-Clarinet-upper-joint.jpg/400px-Buffet-R13-Clarinet-upper-joint.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Buffet-R13-Clarinet-upper-joint.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Upper joint of a Böhm system clarinet</div></div></div>
<p>The main body of most clarinets is divided into the <i>upper joint</i>, the holes and most keys of which are operated by the left hand, and the <i>lower joint</i> with holes and most keys operated by the right hand. Some clarinets have a single joint: on some basset horns and larger clarinets the two joints are held together with a screw clamp and are usually not disassembled for storage. The left thumb operates both a <i>tone hole</i> and the <i>register key</i>. On some models of clarinet, such as many <a href="/wiki/Albert_system" title="Albert system">Albert system</a> clarinets and increasingly some higher-end Böhm system clarinets, the register key is a 'wraparound' key, with the key on the back of the clarinet and the pad on the front. Advocates of the wraparound register key say it improves sound, and it is harder for moisture to accumulate in the tube beneath the pad.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, there is a consensus among repair techs that this type of register key is harder to keep in adjustment, i.e., it is hard to have enough spring pressure to close the hole securely.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The body of a modern soprano clarinet is equipped with numerous <i><a href="/wiki/Tone_hole" title="Tone hole">tone holes</a></i> of which seven (six front, one back) are covered with the fingertips, and the rest are opened or closed using a set of keys. These tone holes let the player produce every note of the chromatic scale. On alto and larger clarinets, and a few soprano clarinets, key-covered holes replace some or all finger holes. The most common system of keys was named the <a href="/wiki/B%C3%B6hm_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Böhm system">Böhm system</a> by its designer <a href="/wiki/Hyacinthe_Klos%C3%A9" title="Hyacinthe Klosé">Hyacinthe Klosé</a> in honour of <a href="/wiki/Flute" title="Flute">flute</a> designer <a href="/wiki/Theobald_B%C3%B6hm" class="mw-redirect" title="Theobald Böhm">Theobald Böhm</a>, but it is not the same as the <a href="/wiki/B%C3%B6hm_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Böhm system">Böhm system</a> used on flutes.<sup id="cite_ref-ridley_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ridley-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The other main system of keys is called the <a href="/wiki/Oehler_system" title="Oehler system">Oehler system</a> and is used mostly in Germany and Austria (see <a href="/wiki/Clarinet#History" title="Clarinet">History</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> The related Albert system is used by some <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Klezmer" title="Klezmer">klezmer</a>, and eastern European folk musicians.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The Albert and Oehler systems are both based on the early <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Mueller" class="mw-redirect" title="Ivan Mueller">Mueller system</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Buffet-R13-Clarinet-lower-joint.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Buffet-R13-Clarinet-lower-joint.jpg/200px-Buffet-R13-Clarinet-lower-joint.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Buffet-R13-Clarinet-lower-joint.jpg/300px-Buffet-R13-Clarinet-lower-joint.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Buffet-R13-Clarinet-lower-joint.jpg/400px-Buffet-R13-Clarinet-lower-joint.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Buffet-R13-Clarinet-lower-joint.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lower Joint of a Böhm system clarinet</div></div></div>
<p>The cluster of keys at the bottom of the upper joint (protruding slightly beyond the cork of the joint) are known as the <i>trill keys</i> and are operated by the right hand.<sup id="cite_ref-pinksterboer_28-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pinksterboer-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> These give the player alternative fingerings that make it easy to play ornaments and <a href="/wiki/Trill_(music)" title="Trill (music)">trills</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pinksterboer_28-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pinksterboer-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The entire weight of the smaller clarinets is supported by the right thumb behind the lower joint on what is called the <i>thumb-rest</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Basset horns and larger clarinets are supported with a neck strap or a floor peg.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Buffet-R13-Clarinet-bell.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Buffet-R13-Clarinet-bell.jpg/200px-Buffet-R13-Clarinet-bell.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Buffet-R13-Clarinet-bell.jpg/300px-Buffet-R13-Clarinet-bell.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Buffet-R13-Clarinet-bell.jpg/400px-Buffet-R13-Clarinet-bell.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Buffet-R13-Clarinet-bell.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bell of a B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> soprano clarinet</div></div></div>
<p>Finally, the flared end is known as the <i>bell</i>. Contrary to popular belief, the bell does not amplify the sound; rather, it improves the uniformity of the instrument's tone for the lowest notes in each register.<sup id="cite_ref-physics_20-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-physics-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> For the other notes, the sound is produced almost entirely at the tone holes, and the bell is irrelevant.<sup id="cite_ref-physics_20-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-physics-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> On <a href="/wiki/Basset-horn" class="mw-redirect" title="Basset-horn">basset horns</a> and larger clarinets, the bell curves up and forward and is usually made of metal.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Keywork">Keywork</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clarinet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Keywork">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Theobald Böhm did not directly invent the key system of the clarinet. Böhm was a flautist who created the key system that is now used for the transverse flute. Klosé and Buffet applied Böhm's system to the clarinet. Although the credit goes to those people, Böhm's name was given to that key system because it was based on that used for flute.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The current Böhm key system consists of generally 6 rings, on the thumb, 1st, 2nd, 4th, 5th, and 6th holes, and a register key just above the thumb hole, easily accessible with the thumb. Above the 1st hole, there is a key that lifts two covers creating the note A in the throat register (high part of low register) of the clarinet. A key at the side of the instrument at the same height as the A key lifts only one of the two covers, producing G<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-sharp">&#x266f;</span></span>, a semitone lower. The A key can be used in conjunction solely with the register key to produce A<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-sharp">&#x266f;</span></span>/B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clarinet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Clarinet_4_key_anon_Bate.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Clarinet_4_key_anon_Bate.jpg/100px-Clarinet_4_key_anon_Bate.jpg" decoding="async" width="100" height="552" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Clarinet_4_key_anon_Bate.jpg/150px-Clarinet_4_key_anon_Bate.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Clarinet_4_key_anon_Bate.jpg/200px-Clarinet_4_key_anon_Bate.jpg 2x" data-file-width="299" data-file-height="1650" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Clarinet_4_key_anon_Bate.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>4-key boxwood clarinet, ca. 1760.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lineage">Lineage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clarinet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Lineage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The clarinet has its roots in the early <a href="/wiki/Single-reed_instrument" title="Single-reed instrument">single-reed instruments</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hornpipe_(musical_instrument)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hornpipe (musical instrument)">hornpipes</a> used in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient  Egypt</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> since the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Albogue" class="mw-redirect" title="Albogue">albogue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alboka" title="Alboka">alboka</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Double_clarinet" title="Double clarinet">double clarinet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The modern clarinet developed from a <a href="/wiki/Baroque_music" title="Baroque music">Baroque</a> instrument called the <a href="/wiki/Chalumeau" title="Chalumeau">chalumeau</a>. This instrument was similar to a <a href="/wiki/Recorder_(musical_instrument)" title="Recorder (musical instrument)">recorder</a>, but with a <a href="/wiki/Single-reed_instrument" title="Single-reed instrument">single-reed</a> mouthpiece and a cylindrical bore.<sup id="cite_ref-karp_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-karp-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Lacking a <a href="/wiki/Register_key" title="Register key">register key</a>, it was played mainly in its fundamental register, with a limited range of about one and a half octaves.<sup id="cite_ref-karp_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-karp-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> It had eight finger holes, like a recorder, and two keys for its two highest notes.<sup id="cite_ref-karp_56-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-karp-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> At this time, contrary to modern practice, the reed was placed in contact with the upper lip.<sup id="cite_ref-karp_56-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-karp-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Around the turn of the 18th century, the chalumeau was modified by converting one of its keys into a register key to produce the first clarinet. This development is usually attributed to German instrument maker <a href="/wiki/Johann_Christoph_Denner" title="Johann Christoph Denner">Johann Christoph Denner</a>, though some have suggested his son Jacob Denner was the inventor.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> This instrument played well in the middle register with a loud, shrill sound, so it was given the name <i>clarinetto</i> meaning "little trumpet" (from <i>clarino</i> + <i>-etto</i>). Early clarinets did not play well in the lower register, so players continued to play the chalumeaux for low notes.<sup id="cite_ref-karp_56-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-karp-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> As clarinets improved, the chalumeau fell into disuse, and these notes became known as the <i>chalumeau register</i>. Original Denner clarinets had two keys, and could play a <a href="/wiki/Chromatic_scale" title="Chromatic scale">chromatic scale</a>, but various makers added more keys to get improved tuning, easier fingerings, and a slightly larger range.<sup id="cite_ref-karp_56-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-karp-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The classical clarinet of <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart">Mozart's</a> day typically had eight finger holes and five keys.
</p><p>Clarinets were soon accepted into orchestras. Later models had a mellower tone than the originals. Mozart (d. 1791) liked the sound of the clarinet (he considered its tone the closest in quality to the human voice) and wrote numerous pieces for the instrument.,<sup id="cite_ref-hacker_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hacker-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> and by the time of <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_van_Beethoven" title="Ludwig van Beethoven">Beethoven</a> (c. 1800–1820), the clarinet was a standard fixture in the orchestra.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pads">Pads</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clarinet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Pads">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The next major development in the history of clarinet was the invention of the modern pad.<sup id="cite_ref-bray_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bray-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Because early clarinets used felt pads to cover the tone holes, they leaked air. This required pad-covered holes to be kept to a minimum, restricting the number of notes the clarinet could play with good tone.<sup id="cite_ref-bray_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bray-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> In 1812, <a href="/wiki/Iwan_M%C3%BCller" title="Iwan Müller">Iwan Müller</a>, a Baltic German community-born clarinetist and inventor, developed a new type of pad that was covered in <a href="/wiki/Leather" title="Leather">leather</a> or <a href="/wiki/Gas_bladder" class="mw-redirect" title="Gas bladder">fish bladder</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jl_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jl-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> It was airtight and let makers increase the number of pad-covered holes. Müller designed a new type of clarinet with seven finger holes and thirteen keys.<sup id="cite_ref-jl_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jl-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> This allowed the instrument to play in any key with near-equal ease. Over the course of the 19th-century, makers made many enhancements to Müller's clarinet, such as the <a href="/wiki/Albert_system" title="Albert system">Albert system</a> and the Baermann system, all keeping the same basic design. Modern instruments may also have cork or synthetic pads.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arrangement_of_keys_and_holes">Arrangement of keys and holes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clarinet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Arrangement of keys and holes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Clarinet_buffet_crampon_rc_prestige_double_dk7216.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Clarinet_buffet_crampon_rc_prestige_double_dk7216.jpg/220px-Clarinet_buffet_crampon_rc_prestige_double_dk7216.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="135" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Clarinet_buffet_crampon_rc_prestige_double_dk7216.jpg/330px-Clarinet_buffet_crampon_rc_prestige_double_dk7216.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Clarinet_buffet_crampon_rc_prestige_double_dk7216.jpg/440px-Clarinet_buffet_crampon_rc_prestige_double_dk7216.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6018" data-file-height="3686" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Clarinet_buffet_crampon_rc_prestige_double_dk7216.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pair of Boehm system soprano clarinets—one in B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> and one in A.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RefBoehm-Satz.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/RefBoehm-Satz.jpg/220px-RefBoehm-Satz.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/RefBoehm-Satz.jpg/330px-RefBoehm-Satz.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/RefBoehm-Satz.jpg/440px-RefBoehm-Satz.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="806" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RefBoehm-Satz.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pair of reform Boehm-clarinets, fully expanded</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Albert_system" title="Albert system">Albert system</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boehm_system_(clarinet)" title="Boehm system (clarinet)">Boehm system (clarinet)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reform_Boehm_system_(clarinet)" title="Reform Boehm system (clarinet)">Reform Boehm system (clarinet)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oehler_system" title="Oehler system">Oehler system</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Clarinets_german.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Clarinets_german.jpg/100px-Clarinets_german.jpg" decoding="async" width="100" height="263" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Clarinets_german.jpg/150px-Clarinets_german.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Clarinets_german.jpg/200px-Clarinets_german.jpg 2x" data-file-width="457" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Clarinets_german.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Oehler_system" title="Oehler system">Oehler system</a> clarinets use additional tone holes to correct intonation (patent C<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-sharp">&#x266f;</span></span>, low E-F correction, fork-F/B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> correction and fork B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> correction)</div></div></div>
<p>The final development in the modern design of the clarinet used in most of the world today was introduced by <a href="/wiki/Hyacinthe_Klos%C3%A9" title="Hyacinthe Klosé">Hyacinthe Klosé</a> in 1839.<sup id="cite_ref-böhm_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-böhm-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> He devised a different arrangement of keys and finger holes, which allow simpler fingering. It was inspired by the <a href="/wiki/Boehm_system" title="Boehm system">Boehm system</a> developed for flutes by <a href="/wiki/Theobald_B%C3%B6hm" class="mw-redirect" title="Theobald Böhm">Theobald Böhm</a>. Klosé was so impressed by Böhm's invention that he named his own system for clarinets the <a href="/wiki/Boehm_system_(clarinet)" title="Boehm system (clarinet)">Boehm system</a>, although it is different from the one used on flutes.<sup id="cite_ref-böhm_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-böhm-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> This new system was slow to gain popularity but gradually became the standard, and today the Boehm system is used everywhere in the world except Germany and Austria. These countries still use a direct descendant of the Mueller clarinet known as the <a href="/wiki/Oehler_system" title="Oehler system">Oehler system</a> clarinet.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Also, some contemporary Dixieland players continue to use <a href="/wiki/Albert_system" title="Albert system">Albert system</a> clarinets.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Other key systems have been developed, many built around modifications to the basic Böhm system:  Full Böhm,<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Mazzeo,<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> McIntyre,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>Benade NX,<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Boehm_system_(clarinet)" title="Reform Boehm system (clarinet)">Reform Boehm system</a> <sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> for example. Each of these addressed—and often improved—issues of particular "weak" tones, or simplified awkward fingerings, but none has caught on widely among players, and the Boehm system remains the standard, to date.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Usage_and_repertoire">Usage and repertoire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clarinet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Usage and repertoire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_of_multiple_clarinets">Use of multiple clarinets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clarinet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Use of multiple clarinets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:72px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Yamaha_clarinet_YCL-CSG-AIII.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Yamaha_clarinet_YCL-CSG-AIII.tif/lossy-page1-70px-Yamaha_clarinet_YCL-CSG-AIII.tif.jpg" decoding="async" width="70" height="656" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Yamaha_clarinet_YCL-CSG-AIII.tif/lossy-page1-105px-Yamaha_clarinet_YCL-CSG-AIII.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Yamaha_clarinet_YCL-CSG-AIII.tif/lossy-page1-140px-Yamaha_clarinet_YCL-CSG-AIII.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="650" data-file-height="6095" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Yamaha_clarinet_YCL-CSG-AIII.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An A clarinet.</div></div></div>
<p>The modern <a href="/wiki/Orchestra" title="Orchestra">orchestral</a> standard of using soprano clarinets in B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> and A has to do partly with the history of the instrument and partly with acoustics, aesthetics, and economics. Before about 1800, due to the lack of airtight pads <i>(see <a href="#History">History</a>)</i>, practical woodwinds could have only a few keys to control accidentals (notes outside their diatonic home scales).<sup id="cite_ref-bray_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bray-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The low (chalumeau) register of the clarinet spans a twelfth (an octave plus a perfect fifth), so the clarinet needs keys/holes to produce all nineteen notes in this range. This involves more keywork than on instruments that "overblow" at the octave—<a href="/wiki/Oboe" title="Oboe">oboes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flute" title="Flute">flutes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bassoon" title="Bassoon">bassoons</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Saxophone" title="Saxophone">saxophones</a>, for example, which need only twelve notes before overblowing. Clarinets with few keys cannot therefore easily play chromatically, limiting any such instrument to a few closely related keys.<sup id="cite_ref-longyear_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-longyear-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> For example, an eighteenth-century clarinet in C could be played in F, C, and G (and their relative minors) with good intonation, but with progressive difficulty and poorer intonation as the key moved away from this range.<sup id="cite_ref-longyear_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-longyear-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> In contrast, for octave-overblowing instruments, an instrument in C with few keys could much more readily be played in any key. This problem was overcome by using three clarinets—in A, B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span>, and C—so that early 19th-century music, which rarely strayed into the remote keys (five or six sharps or flats), could be played as follows: music in 5 to 2 sharps (B major to D major concert pitch) on A clarinet (D major to F major for the player), music in 1 sharp to 1 flat (G to F) on C clarinet, and music in 2 flats to 4 flats (B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> to A<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span>) on the B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> clarinet (C to B<span class="music-symbol" style="font-family: Arial Unicode MS, Lucida Sans Unicode;"><span class="music-flat">&#x266d;</span></span> for the clarinetist). Difficult key signatures and numerous accidentals were thus largely avoided.
